<p><b>Apple Inc.</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational</a> <a href="/wiki/Technology_company" title="Technology company">technology company</a> headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Cupertino,_California" title="Cupertino, California">Cupertino</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, that designs, develops, and sells <a href="/wiki/Consumer_electronics" title="Consumer electronics">consumer electronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">computer software</a>, and online services. Its <a href="/wiki/Computer_hardware" title="Computer hardware">hardware</a> products include the <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a> <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphone</a>, the <a href="/wiki/IPad" title="IPad">iPad</a> <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablet computer</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">Mac</a> <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computer</a>, the <a href="/wiki/IPod" title="IPod">iPod</a> <a href="/wiki/Portable_media_player" title="Portable media player">portable media player</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Apple_Watch" title="Apple Watch">Apple Watch</a> <a href="/wiki/Smartwatch" title="Smartwatch">smartwatch</a>. Apple's consumer software includes the <a href="/wiki/OS_X" title="OS X">OS X</a> and <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a> <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a>, the <a href="/wiki/ITunes" title="ITunes">iTunes</a> <a href="/wiki/Media_player_(software)" title="Media player (software)">media player</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Safari_(web_browser)" title="Safari (web browser)">Safari</a> <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browser</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/ILife" title="ILife">iLife</a> and <a href="/wiki/IWork" title="IWork">iWork</a> creativity and productivity suites. Its online services include the <a href="/wiki/ITunes_Store" title="ITunes Store">iTunes Store</a>, the <a href="/wiki/App_Store_(iOS)" title="App Store (iOS)">iOS App Store</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mac_App_Store" title="Mac App Store">Mac App Store</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ICloud" title="ICloud">iCloud</a>.</p>
<p>Apple was founded by <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">Steve Jobs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Wozniak" title="Steve Wozniak">Steve Wozniak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Wayne" title="Ronald Wayne">Ronald Wayne</a> on April 1, 1976, to develop and sell personal computers.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> It was incorporated as <b>Apple Computer, Inc.</b> on January 3, 1977, and was renamed as Apple Inc. on January 9, 2007, to reflect its shifted focus toward consumer electronics. Apple (<a href="/wiki/NASDAQ" title="NASDAQ">NASDAQ</a>:&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nasdaq.com/symbol/aapl">AAPL</a>) joined the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones Industrial Average</a> on March 19, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Apple is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_largest_information_technology_companies" title="List of the largest information technology companies">world's largest information technology company</a> by revenue, the world's largest technology company by <a href="/wiki/Asset" title="Asset">total assets</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone#Manufacturers" title="Mobile phone">world's second-largest mobile phone manufacturer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> On November 25, 2014, in addition to being the <a href="/wiki/List_of_public_corporations_by_market_capitalization" title="List of public corporations by market capitalization">largest publicly traded corporation in the world by market capitalization</a>, Apple became the first U.S. company to be valued at over <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>700 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> The company employs 115,000 permanent full-time employees as of July 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Apple_Inc.&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-employee_num_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-employee_num-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> and maintains 453 <a href="/wiki/Apple_Store" title="Apple Store">retail stores</a> in sixteen countries as of March 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Apple_Inc.&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>;<sup id="cite_ref-storenum_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-storenum-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> it operates the <a href="/wiki/Apple_Store_(online)" title="Apple Store (online)">online Apple Store</a> and iTunes Store, the latter of which is the world's largest music retailer.</p>
<p>Apple's worldwide annual revenue totaled $233 billion for the <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> ending in September 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-FY_results_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FY_results-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> The company enjoys a high level of <a href="/wiki/Brand_loyalty" title="Brand loyalty">brand loyalty</a> and, according to the 2014 edition of the <a href="/wiki/Interbrand" title="Interbrand">Interbrand</a> Best Global Brands report, is the world's most valuable brand with a valuation of $118.9 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> By the end of 2014, the corporation continued to <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Apple_Inc." title="Criticism of Apple Inc.">receive significant criticism</a> regarding the labor practices of its contractors and its environmental and business practices, including the origins of source materials.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Apple_Inc." title="History of Apple Inc.">History of Apple Inc.</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1976.E2.80.9384:_Founding_and_incorporation">1976–84: Founding and incorporation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Apple_Garage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Home of Paul and Clara Jobs, on Crist Drive in Los Altos, California." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Apple_Garage.jpg/220px-Apple_Garage.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Apple_Garage.jpg/330px-Apple_Garage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Apple_Garage.jpg/440px-Apple_Garage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1365" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Apple_Garage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Home of Paul and Clara Jobs, on Crist Drive in <a href="/wiki/Los_Altos,_California" title="Los Altos, California">Los Altos, California</a>. <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">Steve Jobs</a> formed Apple Computer in its garage with <a href="/wiki/Steve_Wozniak" title="Steve Wozniak">Steve Wozniak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Wayne" title="Ronald Wayne">Ronald Wayne</a> in 1976.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Apple_I.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Apple_I.jpg/220px-Apple_I.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Apple_I.jpg/330px-Apple_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Apple_I.jpg/440px-Apple_I.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="404" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Apple_I.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Apple_I" title="Apple I">Apple I</a>, Apple's first product, was sold as an assembled circuit board and lacked basic features such as a keyboard, monitor, and case. The owner of this unit added a keyboard and a wooden case.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Apple was established on April 1, 1976, by <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">Steve Jobs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Wozniak" title="Steve Wozniak">Steve Wozniak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Wayne" title="Ronald Wayne">Ronald Wayne</a><sup id="cite_ref-AppleConf_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AppleConf-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Benny_Luo_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benny_Luo-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> to sell the <a href="/wiki/Apple_I" title="Apple I">Apple I</a> personal computer kit. The <a href="/wiki/Apple_I" title="Apple I">Apple I</a> kits were computers single handedly designed and hand-built by Wozniak<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> and first shown to the public at the <a href="/wiki/Homebrew_Computer_Club" title="Homebrew Computer Club">Homebrew Computer Club</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The Apple I was sold as a <a href="/wiki/Motherboard" title="Motherboard">motherboard</a> (with <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">CPU</a>, <a href="/wiki/Random-access_memory" title="Random-access memory">RAM</a>, and basic textual-video chips), which was less than what is now considered a complete personal computer.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> The Apple I went on sale in July 1976 and was market-priced at $666.66 ($2,772 in 2016 dollars, adjusted for inflation).<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iWoz_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iWoz-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Apple was incorporated January 3, 1977,<sup id="cite_ref-orgincpr_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orgincpr-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> without Wayne, who sold his share of the company back to Jobs and Wozniak for $800.<sup id="cite_ref-Benny_Luo_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benny_Luo-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Multimillionaire <a href="/wiki/Mike_Markkula" title="Mike Markkula">Mike Markkula</a> provided essential business expertise and funding of $250,000 during the incorporation of Apple.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> During the first five years of operations revenues grew exponentially, doubling about every four months. Between September 1977 and September 1980 yearly sales grew from $775,000 to $118m, an average annual growth rate of 533%.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Apple_II_series" title="Apple II series">Apple II</a>, also invented by Wozniak, was introduced on April 16, 1977, at the first <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Computer_Faire" title="West Coast Computer Faire">West Coast Computer Faire</a>. It differed from its major rivals, the <a href="/wiki/TRS-80" title="TRS-80">TRS-80</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commodore_PET" title="Commodore PET">Commodore PET</a>, because of its character cell-based color graphics and <a href="/wiki/Open_architecture" title="Open architecture">open architecture</a>. While early Apple II models used ordinary cassette tapes as storage devices, they were superseded by the introduction of a 5&#160;1/4&#160;inch <a href="/wiki/Floppy_disk" title="Floppy disk">floppy disk</a> drive and interface called the <a href="/wiki/Disk_II" title="Disk II">Disk II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> The Apple II was chosen to be the desktop platform for the first "<a href="/wiki/Killer_application" title="Killer application">killer app</a>" of the business world: <a href="/wiki/VisiCalc" title="VisiCalc">VisiCalc</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Spreadsheet" title="Spreadsheet">spreadsheet</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_program" title="Computer program">program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lemvc_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lemvc-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> VisiCalc created a business market for the Apple II and gave home users an additional reason to buy an Apple II: compatibility with the office.<sup id="cite_ref-lemvc_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lemvc-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Before VisiCalc, Apple had been a distant third place competitor to <a href="/wiki/Commodore_64" title="Commodore 64">Commodore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tandy_Corporation#Computers" title="Tandy Corporation">Tandy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of the 1970s, Apple had a staff of computer designers and a production line. The company introduced the <a href="/wiki/Apple_III" title="Apple III">Apple III</a> in May 1980 in an attempt to compete with <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> in the business and corporate computing market.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Jobs and several Apple employees, including <a href="/wiki/Jef_Raskin" title="Jef Raskin">Jef Raskin</a>, visited <a href="/wiki/PARC_(company)" title="PARC (company)">Xerox PARC</a> in December 1979 to see the <a href="/wiki/Xerox_Alto" title="Xerox Alto">Xerox Alto</a>. <a href="/wiki/Xerox" title="Xerox">Xerox</a> granted Apple engineers three days of access to the PARC facilities in return for the option to buy 100,000 shares (800,000 split-adjusted shares) of Apple at the pre-IPO price of $10 a share.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jobs was immediately convinced that all future computers would use a graphical user interface (<a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">GUI</a>), and development of a GUI began for the <a href="/wiki/Apple_Lisa" title="Apple Lisa">Apple Lisa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1982, however, he was pushed from the Lisa team due to infighting. Jobs took over Jef Raskin's low-cost-computer project, the <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">Macintosh</a>. A race broke out between the Lisa team and the Macintosh team over which product would ship first. Lisa won the race in 1983 and became the first personal computer sold to the public with a GUI, but was a commercial failure due to its high price tag and limited software titles.<sup id="cite_ref-lemlisa_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lemlisa-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On December 12, 1980, Apple went public at $22 per share,<sup id="cite_ref-FAQ_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> generating more capital than any IPO since <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a> in 1956 and instantly creating more millionaires (about 300) than any company in history.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1984.E2.80.9391:_Success_with_Macintosh">1984–91: Success with Macintosh</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Macintosh_models" title="Timeline of Macintosh models">Timeline of Macintosh models</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Macintosh_128k_transparency.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Macintosh_128k_transparency.png/220px-Macintosh_128k_transparency.png" width="220" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Macintosh_128k_transparency.png/330px-Macintosh_128k_transparency.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Macintosh_128k_transparency.png/440px-Macintosh_128k_transparency.png 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="625" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Macintosh_128k_transparency.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">Macintosh</a>, released in 1984</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ad_apple_1984.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5d/Ad_apple_1984.jpg/220px-Ad_apple_1984.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/5d/Ad_apple_1984.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/5d/Ad_apple_1984.jpg 2x" data-file-width="305" data-file-height="228" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ad_apple_1984.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Apple's <a href="/wiki/1984_(television_commercial)" title="1984 (television commercial)" class="mw-redirect">"1984" television ad</a>, set in a <a href="/wiki/Dystopia" title="Dystopia">dystopian</a> future modeled after the <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> novel <i><a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four" title="Nineteen Eighty-Four">Nineteen Eighty-Four</a></i>, set the tone for the introduction of the Macintosh.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1984, Apple launched the Macintosh, the first personal computer to be sold without a <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming language</a> at all.<sup id="cite_ref-IsProgramingObsolete_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IsProgramingObsolete-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Its debut was signified by <a href="/wiki/1984_(advertisement)" title="1984 (advertisement)">"1984"</a>, a $1.5 million television commercial directed by <a href="/wiki/Ridley_Scott" title="Ridley Scott">Ridley Scott</a> that aired during the third quarter of <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XVIII" title="Super Bowl XVIII">Super Bowl XVIII</a> on January 22, 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> The commercial is now hailed as a watershed event for Apple's success<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> and a "masterpiece".<sup id="cite_ref-masterpiece_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-masterpiece-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cellini_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cellini-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Macintosh initially sold well, but follow-up sales were not strong<sup id="cite_ref-lem1985_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lem1985-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> due to its high price and limited range of software titles. The machine's fortunes changed with the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/LaserWriter" title="LaserWriter">LaserWriter</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/PostScript" title="PostScript">PostScript</a> <a href="/wiki/Laser_printer" title="Laser printer" class="mw-redirect">laser printer</a> to be sold at a reasonable price, and <a href="/wiki/Adobe_PageMaker" title="Adobe PageMaker">PageMaker</a>, an early <a href="/wiki/Desktop_publishing" title="Desktop publishing">desktop publishing</a> package. It has been suggested that the combination of these three products were responsible for the creation of the desktop publishing market.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> The Macintosh was particularly powerful in the desktop publishing market due to its advanced graphics capabilities, which had necessarily been built in to create the intuitive Macintosh GUI.</p>
<p>In 1985, a power struggle developed between Jobs and CEO <a href="/wiki/John_Sculley" title="John Sculley">John Sculley</a>, who had been hired two years earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-lemsculley_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lemsculley-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> The Apple board of directors instructed Sculley to "contain" Jobs and limit his ability to launch expensive forays into untested products. Rather than submit to Sculley's direction, Jobs attempted to oust him from his leadership role at Apple. Sculley found out that Jobs had been attempting to organize a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat#Other_uses_of_the_term" title="Coup d'état">coup</a> and called a board meeting at which Apple's board of directors sided with Sculley and removed Jobs from his managerial duties.<sup id="cite_ref-lem1985_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lem1985-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Jobs resigned from Apple and founded <a href="/wiki/NeXT" title="NeXT">NeXT Inc.</a> the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After Jobs' departure, the Macintosh product line underwent a steady change of focus to higher price points, the so-called "high-right policy" named for the position on a chart of price vs. profits. Jobs had argued the company should produce products aimed at the consumer market and aimed for a $1000 price for the Macintosh, which they were unable to meet. Newer models selling at higher price points offered higher <a href="/wiki/Profit_margin" title="Profit margin">profit margin</a>, and appeared to have no effect on total sales as <a href="/wiki/Power_user" title="Power user">power users</a> snapped up every increase in power. Although some worried about pricing themselves out of the market, the high-right policy was in full force by the mid-1980s, notably due to <a href="/wiki/Jean-Louis_Gass%C3%A9e" title="Jean-Louis Gassée">Jean-Louis Gassée</a>'s mantra of "fifty-five or die", referring to the 55% <a href="/wiki/Profit_margin" title="Profit margin">profit margins</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_II" title="Macintosh II">Macintosh II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>This policy began to backfire in the last years of the decade as new desktop publishing programs appeared on <a href="/wiki/IBM_PC_compatible" title="IBM PC compatible">PC clones</a> that offered some or much of the same functionality of the Macintosh but at far lower price points. The company lost its monopoly in this market, and had already estranged many of its original consumer customer base who could no longer afford their high priced products. The Christmas season of 1989 was the first in the company's history that saw declining sales, and led to a 20% drop in Apple's stock price.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Gassée's objections were overruled, and he was forced from the company in 1990. Later that year, Apple introduced three lower cost models, the <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_Classic" title="Macintosh Classic">Macintosh Classic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_LC" title="Macintosh LC">Macintosh LC</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_IIsi" title="Macintosh IIsi">Macintosh IIsi</a>, all of which saw significant sales due to pent up demand.</p>
<p>In 1991 Apple introduced the <a href="/wiki/PowerBook" title="PowerBook">PowerBook</a>, replacing the "luggable" <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_Portable" title="Macintosh Portable">Macintosh Portable</a> with a design that set the current shape for almost all modern laptops. The same year, Apple introduced <a href="/wiki/System_7" title="System 7">System 7</a>, a major upgrade to the operating system which added color to the interface and introduced new networking capabilities. It remained the architectural basis for <a href="/wiki/Mac_OS" title="Mac OS">Mac OS</a> until 2001. The success of the PowerBook and other products brought increasing revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-lemsculley_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lemsculley-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> For some time, Apple was doing incredibly well, introducing fresh new products and generating increasing profits in the process. The magazine <i><a href="/wiki/MacLife" title="MacLife">MacAddict</a></i> named the period between 1989 and 1991 as the "first golden age" of the Macintosh.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Apple believed the <a href="/wiki/Apple_II_series" title="Apple II series">Apple II series</a> was too expensive to produce and took away sales from the low-end Macintosh.<sup id="cite_ref-apple2history11_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apple2history11-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1990, Apple released the <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_LC" title="Macintosh LC">Macintosh LC</a>, which featured a single <a href="/wiki/Expansion_card" title="Expansion card">expansion slot</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Apple_IIe_Card" title="Apple IIe Card">Apple IIe Card</a> to help migrate Apple II users to the Macintosh platform;<sup id="cite_ref-apple2history11_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apple2history11-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Apple_IIe" title="Apple IIe">Apple IIe</a> was discontinued in 1993.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1991.E2.80.9397:_Decline.2C_restructuring.2C_acquisitions">1991–97: Decline, restructuring, acquisitions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Apple_II_family" title="Timeline of the Apple II family">Timeline of the Apple II family</a></div>
<p>The success of Apple's lower-cost consumer models, especially the LC, also led to cannibalization of their higher priced machines. To address this, management introduced several new brands, selling largely identical machines at different price points aimed at different markets. These were the high-end <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_Quadra" title="Macintosh Quadra">Quadras</a>, the mid-range <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_Centris" title="Macintosh Centris">Centris</a> line, and the ill-fated <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_Performa" title="Macintosh Performa">Performa</a> series. This led to significant market confusion, as customers did not understand the difference between models.<sup id="cite_ref-vawperforma_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vawperforma-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Apple also experimented with a number of other unsuccessful consumer targeted products during the 1990s, including <a href="/wiki/Apple_QuickTake" title="Apple QuickTake">digital cameras</a>, <a href="/wiki/PowerCD" title="PowerCD">portable CD audio players</a>, <a href="/wiki/PowerCD#AppleDesign_Powered_Speakers" title="PowerCD">speakers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Bandai_Pippin" title="Apple Bandai Pippin">video consoles</a>, the <a href="/wiki/EWorld" title="EWorld">eWorld</a> online service, and <a href="/wiki/Apple_Interactive_Television_Box" title="Apple Interactive Television Box">TV appliances</a>. Enormous resources were also invested in the problem-plagued <a href="/wiki/Newton_(platform)" title="Newton (platform)" class="mw-redirect">Newton division</a> based on John Sculley's unrealistic market forecasts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Ultimately, none of these products helped and Apple's market share and stock prices continued to slide.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Through this period, Microsoft continued to gain market share with <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Windows</a> by focusing on delivering software to cheap commodity personal computers, while Apple was delivering a richly engineered but expensive experience.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Apple relied on high profit margins and never developed a clear response; instead, they sued Microsoft for using a GUI similar to the <a href="/wiki/Apple_Lisa" title="Apple Lisa">Apple Lisa</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Apple_Computer,_Inc._v._Microsoft_Corp." title="Apple Computer, Inc. v. Microsoft Corp.">Apple Computer, Inc. v. Microsoft Corp.</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-lemms_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lemms-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> The lawsuit dragged on for years before it was finally dismissed. At this time, a series of major product flops and missed deadlines sullied Apple's reputation, and Sculley was replaced as CEO by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Spindler" title="Michael Spindler">Michael Spindler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newton-IMG_0320_cleanup.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Newton-IMG_0320_cleanup.JPG/220px-Newton-IMG_0320_cleanup.JPG" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Newton-IMG_0320_cleanup.JPG/330px-Newton-IMG_0320_cleanup.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Newton-IMG_0320_cleanup.JPG/440px-Newton-IMG_0320_cleanup.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2382" data-file-height="2048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newton-IMG_0320_cleanup.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Newton_(platform)" title="Newton (platform)" class="mw-redirect">Newton</a> was Apple's first foray into the <a href="/wiki/Personal_digital_assistant" title="Personal digital assistant">PDA</a> markets, as well as one of the first in the industry. Despite being a financial flop at the time of its release, it helped pave the way for the <a href="/wiki/PalmPilot" title="PalmPilot">PalmPilot</a> and Apple's own <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a> and <a href="/wiki/IPad" title="IPad">iPad</a> in the future.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the early 1990s, Apple was developing alternative platforms to the Macintosh, such as <a href="/wiki/A/UX" title="A/UX">A/UX</a>. The Macintosh platform itself was becoming outdated because it was not built for multitasking and because several important software routines were programmed directly into the hardware. In addition, Apple was facing competition from <a href="/wiki/OS/2" title="OS/2">OS/2</a> and <a href="/wiki/UNIX" title="UNIX" class="mw-redirect">UNIX</a> vendors such as <a href="/wiki/Sun_Microsystems" title="Sun Microsystems">Sun Microsystems</a>. The Macintosh would need to be replaced by a new platform or reworked to run on more powerful hardware.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1994, Apple allied with <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motorola" title="Motorola">Motorola</a> in the <a href="/wiki/AIM_alliance" title="AIM alliance">AIM alliance</a> with the goal of creating a new computing platform (the <a href="/wiki/PowerPC_Reference_Platform" title="PowerPC Reference Platform">PowerPC Reference Platform</a>), which would use IBM and Motorola hardware coupled with Apple software. The AIM alliance hoped that PReP's performance and Apple's software would leave the PC far behind and thus counter Microsoft. The same year, Apple introduced the <a href="/wiki/Power_Macintosh" title="Power Macintosh">Power Macintosh</a>, the first of many Apple computers to use Motorola's <a href="/wiki/PowerPC" title="PowerPC">PowerPC</a> processor.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1996, Spindler was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Gil_Amelio" title="Gil Amelio">Gil Amelio</a> as CEO. Amelio made numerous changes at Apple, including extensive layoffs and cut costs.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> After numerous failed attempts to improve Mac OS, first with the <a href="/wiki/Taligent" title="Taligent">Taligent</a> project and later with <a href="/wiki/Copland_(operating_system)" title="Copland (operating system)">Copland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copland_(operating_system)" title="Copland (operating system)">Gershwin</a>, Amelio chose to purchase <a href="/wiki/NeXT" title="NeXT">NeXT</a> and its <a href="/wiki/NeXTSTEP" title="NeXTSTEP">NeXTSTEP</a> operating system and bring Steve Jobs back to Apple.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1997.E2.80.932007:_Return_to_profitability">1997–2007: Return to profitability</span></h3>
<p>The NeXT deal was finalized on February 9, 1997,<sup id="cite_ref-archive_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> bringing Jobs back to Apple as an advisor. On July 9, 1997, Amelio was ousted by the board of directors after overseeing a three-year record-low stock price and crippling financial losses. Jobs acted as the interim CEO and began restructuring the company's product line; it was during this period that he identified the design talent of <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Ive" title="Jonathan Ive">Jonathan Ive</a>, and the pair worked collaboratively to rebuild Apple's status.<sup id="cite_ref-Time_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Time-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the 1997 <a href="/wiki/Macworld/iWorld" title="Macworld/iWorld">Macworld Expo</a>, Jobs announced that Apple would join Microsoft to release new versions of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office" title="Microsoft Office">Microsoft Office</a> for the Macintosh, and that Microsoft had made a $150 million investment in non-voting Apple stock.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> On November 10, 1997, Apple introduced the <a href="/wiki/Apple_Store_(online)" title="Apple Store (online)">Apple Online Store</a>, which was tied to a new build-to-order manufacturing strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On August 15, 1998, Apple introduced a new all-in-one computer reminiscent of the <a href="/wiki/Macintosh_128K" title="Macintosh 128K">Macintosh 128K</a>: the <a href="/wiki/IMac_G3" title="IMac G3">iMac</a>. The iMac design team was led by Ive, who would later design the <a href="/wiki/IPod" title="IPod">iPod</a> and the <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-levgrossman_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-levgrossman-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> The iMac featured modern technology and a unique design, and sold almost 800,000 units in its first five months.<sup id="cite_ref-800kimacs_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-800kimacs-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During this period,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2014)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> Apple completed numerous acquisitions to create a portfolio of digital production software for both professionals and consumers. In 1998, Apple purchased <a href="/wiki/Macromedia" title="Macromedia">Macromedia</a>'s Key Grip software project, signaling an expansion into the <a href="/wiki/Digital_video" title="Digital video">digital video</a> editing market. The sale was an outcome of Macromedia's decision to solely focus upon web development software. The product, still unfinished at the time of the sale, was renamed "<a href="/wiki/Final_Cut_Pro" title="Final Cut Pro">Final Cut Pro</a>" when it was launched on the retail market in April 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> The development of Key Grip also led to Apple's release of the consumer video-editing product <a href="/wiki/IMovie" title="IMovie">iMovie</a> in October 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-Bell_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bell-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Next, Apple successfully acquired the German company Astarte, which had developed <a href="/wiki/DVD_authoring" title="DVD authoring">DVD authoring</a> technology, as well as Astarte's corresponding products and engineering team in April 2000. Astarte's digital tool DVDirector was subsequently transformed into the professional-oriented <a href="/wiki/DVD_Studio_Pro" title="DVD Studio Pro">DVD Studio Pro</a> software product. Apple then employed the same technology to create <a href="/wiki/IDVD" title="IDVD">iDVD</a> for the consumer market.<sup id="cite_ref-Bell_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bell-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2002, Apple purchased <a href="/wiki/Nothing_Real" title="Nothing Real">Nothing Real</a> for their advanced digital <a href="/wiki/Compositing" title="Compositing">compositing</a> application <a href="/wiki/Shake_(software)" title="Shake (software)">Shake</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Emagic" title="Emagic">Emagic</a> for the music productivity application <a href="/wiki/Logic_Pro" title="Logic Pro">Logic</a>. The purchase of Emagic made Apple the first computer manufacturer to own a music software company. The acquisition was followed by the development of Apple's consumer-level <a href="/wiki/GarageBand" title="GarageBand">GarageBand</a> application.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> The release of <a href="/wiki/IPhoto" title="IPhoto">iPhoto</a> in the same year completed the <a href="/wiki/ILife" title="ILife">iLife</a> suite.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X" title="Mac OS X" class="mw-redirect">Mac OS X</a>, based on NeXT's <a href="/wiki/OpenStep" title="OpenStep">OPENSTEP</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Software_Distribution" title="Berkeley Software Distribution">BSD Unix</a>, was released on March 24, 2001 after several years of development. Aimed at consumers and professionals alike, Mac OS X aimed to combine the stability, reliability and security of <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a> with the ease of use afforded by an overhauled user interface. To aid users in migrating from <a href="/wiki/Mac_OS_9" title="Mac OS 9">Mac OS 9</a>, the new operating system allowed the use of OS 9 applications within Mac OS X via the <a href="/wiki/Classic_Environment" title="Classic Environment">Classic Environment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Line_at_the_opening_of_first_Apple_Store_in_America.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Line_at_the_opening_of_first_Apple_Store_in_America.jpg/220px-Line_at_the_opening_of_first_Apple_Store_in_America.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Line_at_the_opening_of_first_Apple_Store_in_America.jpg/330px-Line_at_the_opening_of_first_Apple_Store_in_America.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Line_at_the_opening_of_first_Apple_Store_in_America.jpg/440px-Line_at_the_opening_of_first_Apple_Store_in_America.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="384" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Line_at_the_opening_of_first_Apple_Store_in_America.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Apple_retail_store" title="Apple retail store" class="mw-redirect">Apple retail stores</a> allow potential customers to use <a href="/wiki/Floor_model" title="Floor model">floor models</a> without making a purchase.<br />
(Line at the opening of the first Apple Store; <a href="/wiki/Tysons_Corner_Center" title="Tysons Corner Center">Tysons Corner Center</a>, Virginia on May 19, 2001.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On May 19, 2001, Apple opened the first official <a href="/wiki/Apple_Store_(retail)" title="Apple Store (retail)" class="mw-redirect">Apple Retail Stores</a> in Virginia and California.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> On October 23 of the same year, Apple debuted the <a href="/wiki/IPod" title="IPod">iPod</a> portable digital audio player. The product, which was first sold on November 10, 2001, was phenomenally successful with over 100 million units sold within six years.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2003, Apple's <a href="/wiki/ITunes_Store" title="ITunes Store">iTunes Store</a> was introduced. The service offered online <a href="/wiki/Music_download" title="Music download">music downloads</a> for $0.99 a song and integration with the iPod. The iTunes store quickly became the market leader in online music services, with over 5 billion downloads by June 19, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apple%27s_transition_to_Intel_processors" title="Apple's transition to Intel processors">Apple's transition to Intel processors</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MacBook_Pro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/MacBook_Pro.jpg/220px-MacBook_Pro.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/MacBook_Pro.jpg/330px-MacBook_Pro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/MacBook_Pro.jpg/440px-MacBook_Pro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1623" data-file-height="1305" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MacBook_Pro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/MacBook_Pro" title="MacBook Pro">MacBook Pro</a>, Apple's first laptop with an <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> microprocessor, introduced in January 2006.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Worldwide_Developers_Conference" title="Worldwide Developers Conference" class="mw-redirect">Worldwide Developers Conference</a> keynote address on June 6, 2005, Jobs announced that Apple would begin producing <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a>-based Mac computers in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-printel_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-printel-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> On January 10, 2006, the new <a href="/wiki/MacBook_Pro" title="MacBook Pro">MacBook Pro</a> and <a href="/wiki/IMac" title="IMac">iMac</a> became the first Apple computers to use Intel's <a href="/wiki/Intel_Core#Core_Duo" title="Intel Core">Core Duo</a> CPU. By August 7, 2006, Apple made the transition to Intel chips for the entire Mac product line—over one year sooner than announced.<sup id="cite_ref-printel_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-printel-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> The Power Mac, iBook and PowerBook brands were retired during the transition; the <a href="/wiki/Mac_Pro" title="Mac Pro">Mac Pro</a>, <a href="/wiki/MacBook" title="MacBook">MacBook</a>, and MacBook Pro became their respective successors.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> On April 29, 2009, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> reported that Apple was building its own team of engineers to design microchips.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Apple also introduced <a href="/wiki/Boot_Camp_(software)" title="Boot Camp (software)">Boot Camp</a> in 2006 to help users install <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP" title="Windows XP">Windows XP</a> or <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Windows Vista</a> on their Intel Macs alongside Mac OS X.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Apple's success during this period was evident in its stock price. Between early 2003 and 2006, the price of Apple's stock increased more than tenfold, from around $6 per share (split-adjusted) to over $80. In January 2006, Apple's <a href="/wiki/Market_cap" title="Market cap" class="mw-redirect">market cap</a> surpassed that of <a href="/wiki/Dell" title="Dell">Dell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-modell_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modell-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Nine years prior, Dell's CEO <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dell" title="Michael Dell">Michael Dell</a> had said that if he ran Apple he would "shut it down and give the money back to the shareholders."<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Apple's market share in computers had grown, it remained far behind competitors using Microsoft Windows, accounting for about 8% of desktops and laptops in the US.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Since 2001, Apple's design team has progressively abandoned the use of translucent colored plastics first used in the <a href="/wiki/IMac_G3" title="IMac G3">iMac G3</a>. This design change began with the <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>-made <a href="/wiki/PowerBook" title="PowerBook">PowerBook</a> and was followed by the <a href="/wiki/IBook" title="IBook">iBook</a>'s white <a href="/wiki/Polycarbonate" title="Polycarbonate">polycarbonate</a> structure and the flat-panel <a href="/wiki/IMac" title="IMac">iMac</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2007.E2.80.9311:_Success_with_mobile_devices">2007–11: Success with mobile devices</span></h3>
<p>During his keynote speech at the Macworld Expo on January 9, 2007, Jobs announced that Apple Computer, Inc. would thereafter be known as "Apple Inc.", because the company had shifted its emphasis from computers to consumer electronics.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> This event also saw the announcement of the <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Apple_TV" title="Apple TV">Apple TV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MW2007_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW2007-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> The following day, Apple shares hit $97.80, an all-time high at that point. In May, Apple's share price passed the $100 mark.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Apple would achieve widespread success with its iPhone, <a href="/wiki/IPod_Touch" title="IPod Touch">iPod Touch</a> and <a href="/wiki/IPad" title="IPad">iPad</a> products, which introduced innovations in <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portable_music_player" title="Portable music player" class="mw-redirect">portable music players</a> and <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computers</a> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> Furthermore, by early 2007, 800,000 Final Cut Pro users were registered.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In an article posted on Apple's website on February 6, 2007, Jobs wrote that Apple would be willing to sell music on the iTunes Store without <a href="/wiki/Digital_rights_management" title="Digital rights management">digital rights management</a> (DRM), thereby allowing tracks to be played on third-party players, if record labels would agree to drop the technology.<sup id="cite_ref-NewsMax_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewsMax-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> On April 2, 2007, Apple and <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">EMI</a> jointly announced the removal of DRM technology from EMI's catalog in the iTunes Store, effective in May 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Other record labels eventually followed suit and Apple published a press release in January 2009 to announce the corresponding changes to the iTunes Store.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2008, Apple launched the <a href="/wiki/App_Store_(iOS)" title="App Store (iOS)">App Store</a> to sell third-party applications for the <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a> and <a href="/wiki/IPod_Touch" title="IPod Touch">iPod Touch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Within a month, the store sold 60 million applications and registered an average daily revenue of $1 million, with Jobs speculating in August 2008 that the App Store could become a billion-dollar business for Apple.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> By October 2008, Apple was the third-largest mobile handset supplier in the world due to the popularity of the iPhone.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On December 16, 2008, Apple announced that 2009 would be the last year the corporation would attend the Macworld Expo, after more than 20 years of attendance, and that senior vice president of Worldwide Product Marketing <a href="/wiki/Philip_W._Schiller" title="Philip W. Schiller" class="mw-redirect">Philip Schiller</a> would deliver the 2009 keynote address in lieu of the expected Jobs. The official press release explained that Apple was "scaling back" on trade shows in general, including Macworld Tokyo and the Apple Expo in Paris, France, primarily because the enormous successes of the Apple Retail Stores and website had rendered trade shows a minor promotional channel.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On January 14, 2009, an internal memo from Jobs announced that he would be taking a six-month medical <a href="/wiki/Leave_of_absence" title="Leave of absence">leave of absence</a> from Apple until the end of June 2009 and would spend the time focusing on his health. In the email, Jobs stated that "the curiosity over my personal health continues to be a distraction not only for me and my family, but everyone else at Apple as well", and explained that the break would allow the company "to focus on delivering extraordinary products".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite Jobs's absence, Apple recorded its best non-holiday quarter (Q1 FY 2009) during the <a href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" title="Late-2000s recession" class="mw-redirect">recession</a> with revenue of $8.16 billion and profit of $1.21 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Apple-Inc-Apr-2009-10-Q_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apple-Inc-Apr-2009-10-Q-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/40px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png" width="40" height="22" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/60px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/80px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="759" data-file-height="415" /></td>
